    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2477477703" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2477477703" />
    <a title="Ilana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59371159-ilana"><img alt="Ilana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563253235p2/59371159.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2477477703?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ilana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ilana" href="/user/show/59371159-ilana">Ilana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782070409303" href="/book/show/854442.Au_Bonheur_des_Dames">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=en-francais">en-francais</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=library-book">library-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2477477703" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16054390821708187294">A young girl called Denise Baudu arrives in Paris from the provinces with her two younger brothers to look for work, following the death of their parents. She pays a visit to her uncle, who owns a notions shop, hoping she can count on him to put them up and give her work, having previous experience as a salesgirl. But her uncle's business is hurting, as are all the other small specialty shops on the street, and he can't give her work, nor can any of the other local respectable family businesses,</span>
  <span id="freeText16054390821708187294" style="display:none">A young girl called Denise Baudu arrives in Paris from the provinces with her two younger brothers to look for work, following the death of their parents. She pays a visit to her uncle, who owns a notions shop, hoping she can count on him to put them up and give her work, having previous experience as a salesgirl. But her uncle's business is hurting, as are all the other small specialty shops on the street, and he can't give her work, nor can any of the other local respectable family businesses, thanks to the relentless growth of what also started as a small shop but has now grown into a huge new department store, <i>
  <b>The Ladies' Paradise</b>
</i> of the title. <br /><br />We'd met the owner of the department store, Octave Mouret, in the previous novel <i>Pot Luck</i>, in which he himself had just arrived from the provinces, filled with dreams and counting on making them come true by seducing rich women willing to part with some cash to secure his charms. Now he is a young and rich widower, and with his innovative big business ideas, has made a huge success of his department store which is turning into a veritable empire, which all the wealthy women of Paris flock to for the large selection of merchandise and the pull of strategical low pricing. Mouret prides himself on having used women to succeed in life, and now continues to take pride in the fact that <i>all</i> women are now at his mercy, since he knows how to manipulate their desires to turn them into dedicated customers. Denise finds work there, since it is the biggest employer in the area, and while she suffers the unending taunts and malice of her coworkers for being the unsophisticated newcomer from the provinces, she's immediately drawn to Mouret, whom she admires for his bold business ideas. But unlike the other salesgirls who are only too glad to supplement their meagre income with extras from lovers on the side—while hoping Mouret will choose them as his next playthings with all the bonuses that implies—, Denise is determined she won't give herself away at any price. This leads Mouret to rue his success which brings him all the riches in the world, but not the one woman he progressively and predictably enough becomes completed obsessed with (being the only one who won't abandon herself to him, naturally). <br /><br />The struggle between the small business owners and a giant superstore were a relatively new phenomenon when this novel was published in 1883, and though this market dynamic is old hat to us in the 21st century, I still found it to be one of the most poignant parts of the story. The trouble I had with this novel is there is very little story or character development, since the Ladies' Paradise <i>itself</i> is the main protagonist, and Zola, in his characteristic way, depicts it in lavish detail, with countless descriptions of the merchandise and displays, and the inner workings of the store and rivalries and gossip among what eventually grow to be thousands of employees. This in itself would have been interesting, but the lists of items described went on endlessly, so that very little happened story wise in the process. There is an endlessly drawn out paroxysm of the biggest display of all the tints of white merchandise ever seen in Paris before, which I believe went on for three chapters. It all sounded very very pretty, but really tiresome in the end, much like an entire day spent shopping, something I've grown jaded to, which must show my middle age. Very glad I've finally finished this one and can now move on to the other books in the series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16054390821708187294" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_182159314" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182159314" />
    <a title="Jane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2050107-jane"><img alt="Jane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509633087p2/2050107.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/182159314?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jane" href="/user/show/2050107-jane">Jane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2050107-jane?shelf=borrowed">borrowed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer182159314" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3845634001786829646">The Ladies’ Paradise was one of those books I meant to read, expected to love, but never quite got to for a long, long time. Now I have finished reading, I am inclined to say that the verdict is flawed but fabulous.<br/> <br/>Let me explain.<br/> <br/>The story begins with twenty year-old Denise Baudu and her two young brothers arriving in Paris from the country. Denise has done her best for her brothers since their parents died, but she was struggling, and so she came to Paris to take up the offer of help and supp</span>
  <span id="freeText3845634001786829646" style="display:none">The Ladies’ Paradise was one of those books I meant to read, expected to love, but never quite got to for a long, long time. Now I have finished reading, I am inclined to say that the verdict is flawed but fabulous.<br /> <br />Let me explain.<br /> <br />The story begins with twenty year-old Denise Baudu and her two young brothers arriving in Paris from the country. Denise has done her best for her brothers since their parents died, but she was struggling, and so she came to Paris to take up the offer of help and support that her uncle had offered.<br /> <br />I was immediately pulled in by the storytelling, and I worried that maybe that offer was the kind you make but expect never to be taken up. And indeed it was. It wasn’t that he didn’t want to help but he was struggling, his small shop losing business to the expanding department store across the street: The Ladies’ Paradise.<br /> <br />And that presented Denise with a problem: she had to work, but the only work available was at The Ladies’ Paradise. The establishment hated and resented by her uncle and his neighbours.<br /> <br />She understood their feelings, but she had to work, and she was drawn to dazzling emporium. Denise secured a job. And she stepped into The Ladies’ Paradise.<br /> <br />There is so much to say about The Ladies’ Paradise.<br /> <br />It is an almost magical emporium, a huge department store that grew from a small draper’s shop, packed full of seductive colours, fabrics, clothes, furnishing, and so much more. The descriptions are rich, detailed, and utterly captivating.<br /> <br />It draws in the ladies of Paris very cleverly, with carefully planned layouts, seasonal sales, attentive service, such well thought out, modern marketing. So much modernity, but behind the scenes it was rather different. For the staff it was not so very different from life in service in a big house. They lived in dormitories, ate in a canteen, had little time of their own, and had to work, work, work to secure the commission they so desperately needed and to hold on to their jobs. <br /><br />Denise struggled at first, and she was easy prey for ambitious, ruthless salesgirls. But she knew she had to support her family, she held on to her principles, and, though there were many setbacks, in time she would rise through the ranks.<br /> <br />And Denise caught the eye of Octave Mouret: the creator, the owner of The Ladies’ Paradise. A man who knows how to seduce women, in his private life and in his wonderous emporium. But Denise is the woman who will not be seduced. And of course, that makes her all the more fascinating …<br /> <br />The Ladies’ Paradise held me from start to finish. With wonderful, readable storytelling. With rich descriptions, and so, so many details. And with some quite extraordinary set-pieces.<br /> <br />I’m afraid that the characters didn’t quite live up to all of that. The leads were a little too predictable, a little too straightforward, and the supporting cast a little too one-dimensional. And the view of human nature was a little bleak. So many thoughtless, selfish people.<br /> <br />But I loved watching the social changes that the department store was bringing, and I was captivated by the nicely predictable love story.<br /> <br />And now I am wondering which of Zola’s works to read next.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3845634001786829646" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_723627306" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/723627306" />
    <a title="Diane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6180218-diane"><img alt="Diane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373177864p2/6180218.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/723627306?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/6180218-diane">Diane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer723627306" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11241852947833282948">It was with fear and trepidation that I started Zola's "Ladies' Delight" -<br/>I was still reeling from "Therese Raquin". I desperately wanted a strong,<br/>good woman character with a positive ending. This would have to be one<br/>of Zola's more accessible books (is this phase still used) as it plots the<br/>course of beautiful, determined and honourable Denise, a real worker and<br/>in her way, a visionary.<br/>Orphaned and penniless Denise and her little brother Jean arrive on the<br/>doorstep of their Uncle Baudu, hoping to b</span>
  <span id="freeText11241852947833282948" style="display:none">It was with fear and trepidation that I started Zola's "Ladies' Delight" -<br />I was still reeling from "Therese Raquin". I desperately wanted a strong,<br />good woman character with a positive ending. This would have to be one<br />of Zola's more accessible books (is this phase still used) as it plots the<br />course of beautiful, determined and honourable Denise, a real worker and<br />in her way, a visionary.<br />Orphaned and penniless Denise and her little brother Jean arrive on the<br />doorstep of their Uncle Baudu, hoping to be given work and shelter but their<br />uncle's shop, a run down haberdashery is under the shadow of "Ladies' Delight",<br />a looming department store that even as the story starts is planning on <br />expansion.<br />Interesting thing, a few months ago there was an English serial on TV called<br />"The Paradise" - it was a very loose adaptation of "Ladies' Delight", not that<br />you'd recognize it - all style and no substance. Instead of France it was set<br />in the north of England and the sub plot that made Zola's novel great, the<br />smaller shops being sucked into the vortex of the huge department store's<br />tentacles was almost missing!! Another missing part was the sub-plot involving<br />dissolute Jean - it seems Zola must always have a despicable man among his<br />characters. It is on account of Jean's recklessness that they seek out their<br />Uncle. Even thought Jean has not been paid for his apprenticeship to a cabinet<br />maker in their home town, he has developed a taste for the finer things of life<br />- due to his scandalous affair with a young girl. And for most of the book his<br />extravagances push Denise to brink of penury and despair.<br />Denise, in desperation, gets a job in "The Ladies' Delight" - much to her Uncle's<br />disgust but it is here that her hard work and organisational skills bring her to<br />the notice of Mouret, the all powerful owner. Mouret is a lady's man and has<br />current and discarded mistresses among the shop girls - he becomes very interested<br />in her but Denise's only interest is in getting on. Another striking feature of<br />the book is the lush descriptions of the fabrics and the interior of the shop -<br />the sumptuous furnishings and of how the "lay out" of the shop begins to take<br />place. In dire contrast with the awfulness and dirt and grime of the street where<br />other shop owners are driven mad as they are engulfed by this light emblazoned<br />monster.<br />Definitely I would recommend this as an introduction to Zola - for once a heroine<br />who is resourceful and virtuous and keeps these qualities to give her a happy<br />ending.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11241852947833282948" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_475081575" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/475081575" />
    <a title="Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9421563-maria"><img alt="Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567662931p2/9421563.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/475081575?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria" href="/user/show/9421563-maria">Maria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer475081575" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3737067315022103169">I had high expectations on this novel. Could have something to do with BBC's adaptation. I won't say I was disappointed but I wasn't really happy either. I blame BBC.<br/><br/>The novel is set in the late 1900 century Paris. Denise and her two younger brothers comes to Paris to make a living after their parent's death. She was hoping to get help from her uncle, but as it turns out, a large department store has opened just opposite the uncle's shop and all the smaller shops around this "monstrosity" are on</span>
  <span id="freeText3737067315022103169" style="display:none">I had high expectations on this novel. Could have something to do with BBC's adaptation. I won't say I was disappointed but I wasn't really happy either. I blame BBC.<br /><br />The novel is set in the late 1900 century Paris. Denise and her two younger brothers comes to Paris to make a living after their parent's death. She was hoping to get help from her uncle, but as it turns out, a large department store has opened just opposite the uncle's shop and all the smaller shops around this "monstrosity" are on the brink of bankruptcy.<br /><br />Monsieur Mouret is the genius behind this "monstrosity", that he has created to "seduce" his female costumers. When Denise walks into his life everything changes. He who is used to always get what he wants, suddenly gets a no. He can't even understand why he wants her so, it can't only be because she's the only one who has turned him down. Eventually he throws himself at her feet, begging her to love him. The only thing I can think is "how on earth has she got the heart to say no?" and not only once but lots of times. This makes my reading a bit annoyed and hastened, but I did get to enjoy Zola's vivid language and sometimes too embellished and detailed accounts of dresses and buildings.<br /><br />In BBC's adaptation, the setting is moved to England and Monsieur Mouret is now Mr Morey and played by Emun Elliot (Game of Thrones and Prometheus) who is perfect for the role as the seductive store owner. Denise (Joanna Vanderham) comes to town, without brothers here, and falls head over heels for Morey (who wouldn't?), but keeps fighting the it and not fall to temptation. Denise takes more room in the adaptation, and she's not as teased as in the novel. It's thanks to the adaptation, which I happened upon on facebook (the great think about following pages such as British period drama who tells you about new great series!), that I found the novel and I'm thankful! I do hope the tv-series will find it's way to Sweden, so that all of you, who hasn't BBC on their tv box or is skulking around the net to find streaming services, can also enjoy this wonderful tv-series. Until then, you can always read the book!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3737067315022103169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_264293378" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/264293378" />
    <a title="Individualfrog" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6433504-individualfrog"><img alt="Individualfrog" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316894675p2/6433504.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/264293378?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Individualfrog" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Individualfrog" href="/user/show/6433504-individualfrog">Individualfrog</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143124702" href="/book/show/18249283-the-paradise">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6433504-individualfrog?shelf=opinionated-digressive-fiction">opinionated-digressive-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6433504-individualfrog?shelf=life-as-i-want-to-live-it">life-as-i-want-to-live-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer264293378" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5083153554268747530">As I work in a department store in real life, I can tell you that nothing has changed, except that the giant megastores now crushing the little guy are the even more soulless and vile Amazon and Walmart. The department stores, which are now sort of the little guys getting crushed, have mostly changed for the worse, although it is nice that we don't work 13 hour days anymore. The main thing, as Zola emphasizes (unlike any contemporary I've ever read), is that commission-based pay is a tool for de</span>
  <span id="freeText5083153554268747530" style="display:none">As I work in a department store in real life, I can tell you that nothing has changed, except that the giant megastores now crushing the little guy are the even more soulless and vile Amazon and Walmart. The department stores, which are now sort of the little guys getting crushed, have mostly changed for the worse, although it is nice that we don't work 13 hour days anymore. The main thing, as Zola emphasizes (unlike any contemporary I've ever read), is that commission-based pay is a tool for destroying solidarity and making workers blame each other, instead of the boss, for their shitty pay. Thank god I don't work on commission, but everyone who does hates everybody else, and they never think to point the finger at anyone but their equally oppressed peers.<br /><br />As I expected, this book is at its best when presenting an overflowing banquet of description of luxury, excess, sensory overload -- the goods, the displays, the crowds, the perks and advertisements. The love story is a bust, in an unexpected way. I think of Zola as a frank, unromantic realist; but in this book the heroine wins all through the extremely Victorian means of jealously guarding her chastity, and gets the guy through the magical power of her spotless virginity. Who got this Dickens in my Zola? She's only interesting when she's guiltily rapturous about the glories of modern capitalism, which, I would guess from the context, probably reflects Zola's own feelings -- "it sucks but it's necessary". Which is also a bit disappointing, but less dreary than the socialist sort of social realist books that I agree with more politically.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5083153554268747530" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_966881418" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/966881418" />
    <a title="Katrina Passick Lumsden" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1964146-katrina-passick-lumsden"><img alt="Katrina Passick Lumsden" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1416959676p2/1964146.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/966881418?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katrina Passick Lumsden" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katrina Passick Lumsden" href="/user/show/1964146-katrina-passick-lumsden">Katrina Passick Lumsden</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19297908-the-paradise">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer966881418" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13221343863448196279">To say Emile Zola had a way with words would be an insulting understatement. This is a great story, a study of the effects of capitalism as well as a study of human behavior. My only complaint would be that Zola was oftentimes a bit <i>too</i> wordy. Setting that aside, I was fascinated by the portrayal of the rise of the first department store in France and the effect it had on its section of Paris. Zola managed to present it in such a way wherein both parties (the big store "counter jumpers" and the </span>
  <span id="freeText13221343863448196279" style="display:none">To say Emile Zola had a way with words would be an insulting understatement. This is a great story, a study of the effects of capitalism as well as a study of human behavior. My only complaint would be that Zola was oftentimes a bit <i>too</i> wordy. Setting that aside, I was fascinated by the portrayal of the rise of the first department store in France and the effect it had on its section of Paris. Zola managed to present it in such a way wherein both parties (the big store "counter jumpers" and the small business mom and pop stores) are guilty and innocent. He tells the story without asking the reader to choose a side because, ultimately, both sides are simultaneously right and wrong. <br /><br />It's a long book, so don't expect to finish it in one night. I came close only because I'm a fast reader and the tension between Denise and Mouret had me scrambling to see what happened between them. The ending was a bit too abrupt for my taste, but hey, you can't have everything. And while Mouret was often infuriating with his almost ironic misogyny, his quick wit and passion for life bleed through the page. One can't help but feel like they want to be friends with him even while he's using people and discarding them like tissues. <br /><br /><b>"What! Do I enjoy myself! What's this nonsense you're saying? You're in a sorry state! Of course I enjoy myself, even when things go wrong, because then I'm furious at seeing them go wrong. I'm a passionate fellow; I don't take life calmly, and perhaps that's just why I'm interested in it."</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="13221343863448196279" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2434454097" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2434454097" />
    <a title="Gwen Cooper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2525333-gwen-cooper"><img alt="Gwen Cooper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1518218244p2/2525333.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2434454097?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gwen Cooper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gwen Cooper" href="/user/show/2525333-gwen-cooper">Gwen Cooper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2434454097" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4955886276796923958">A total Cinderella story set against the backdrop of shopping (!!!) in Paris (!!!)--maybe I'm the girliest girl who ever girled, but I absolutely loved this book. In addition to the romance plot and gorgeous descriptions of 19th Century silks and satins, this was also a fascinating look back at the world's very first department store and the origins of many of the commercial conventions (sales commissions, markdowns, gifts-with-purchase) that we take for granted today. A great summer read!</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Nd554UPK6Evmj3N/EBrwZqMUerL6mAXLCA0V0US77kes9JQf0lGvGjY8N6if32YXbDmc0URj4R19kQUHtbWM/Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_780581'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/780581.Bel_Ami?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Bel-Ami<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/18791.Guy_de_Maupassant?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Guy de Maupassant<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.85 avg rating &mdash; 29,553 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1885\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer10245484588671482905\">Guy de Maupassant\'s scandalous tale of an opportunistic young man corrupted by the allure of power, \"Bel-Ami\" is translated with an introduction by Douglas Parmee in \"Penguin Classics\". Young, attractive and very ambitious, George Duroy, known to his<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10245484588671482905\" style=\"display:none\">Guy de Maupassant\'s scandalous tale of an opportunistic young man corrupted by the allure of power, &quot;Bel-Ami&quot; is translated with an introduction by Douglas Parmee in &quot;Penguin Classics&quot;. Young, attractive and very ambitious, George Duroy, known to his admirers as Bel-Ami, is offered a job as a journalist on La Vie francaise and soon makes a great success of his new career. But he also comes face to face with the realities of the corrupt society in which he lives - the sleazy colleagues, the manipulative mistresses, and wily financiers - and swiftly learns to become an arch-seducer, blackmailer and social climber in a world where love is only a means to an end. Written when Maupassant was at the height of his powers, &quot;Bel-Ami&quot; is a novel of great frankness and cynicism, but it is also infused with the sheer joy of life - depicting the scenes and characters of Paris in the belle epoque with wit, sensitivity, and humanity. Douglas Parmee\'s translation captures all the vigour and vitality of Maupassant\'s novel. His introduction explores the similarities between Bel-Ami and Maupassant himself and demonstrates the skill with which the author depicts his large cast of characters and the French society of the Toujours il sera surpris, réveillé, entraîné dans un véritable enchantement d\'intelligence et d\'humour.\n\nCe livre est la transcription de vingt-cinq émissions proposées par l\'auteur sur France       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4750._mile_Zola">About Émile Zola</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4750._mile_Zola"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1544927603p3/4750.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4750._mile_Zola">Émile Zola
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,111
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2148379931355451369">Émile François Zola was an influential French novelist, the most important example of the literary school of naturalism, and a major figure in the political liberalization of France.<br/><br/>More than half of Zola's novels were part of a set of 20 books collectively known as Les Rougon-Macquart. Unlike Balzac who in the midst of his literary career resynthesized his work into La Comédie Humaine, Zola from </span>
  <span id="freeText2148379931355451369" style="display:none">Émile François Zola was an influential French novelist, the most important example of the literary school of naturalism, and a major figure in the political liberalization of France.<br /><br />More than half of Zola's novels were part of a set of 20 books collectively known as Les Rougon-Macquart. Unlike Balzac who in the midst of his literary career resynthesized his work into La Comédie Humaine, Zola from the start at the age of 28 had thought of the complete layout of the series. Set in France's Second Empire, the series traces the "environmental" influences of violence, alcohol and prostitution which became more prevalent during the second wave of the Industrial Revolution. The series examines two branches of a family: the respectable (that is, legitimate) Rougons and the disreputable (illegitimate) Macquarts for five generations.<br /><br />As he described his plans for the series, "I want to portray, at the outset of a century of liberty and truth, a family that cannot restrain itself in its rush to possess all the good things that progress is making available and is derailed by its own momentum, the fatal convulsions that accompany the birth of a new world."<br /><br />Although Zola and Cézanne were friends from childhood, they broke in later life over Zola's fictionalized depiction of Cézanne and the Bohemian life of painters in his novel <i>L'Œuvre</i> (<i>The Masterpiece</i>, 1886).<br /><br />From 1877 with the publication of <i>L'Assommoir</i>, Émile Zola became wealthy, he was better paid than Victor Hugo, for example. He became a figurehead among the literary bourgeoisie and organized cultural dinners with Guy de Maupassant, Joris-Karl Huysmans and other writers at his luxurious villa in Medan near Paris after 1880. <i>Germinal</i> in 1885, then the three 'cities', <i>Lourdes</i> in 1894, <i>Rome</i> in 1896 and <i>Paris</i> in 1897, established Zola as a successful author.<br /><br />The self-proclaimed leader of French naturalism, Zola's works inspired operas such as those of Gustave Charpentier, notably <i>Louise</i> in the 1890s. His works, inspired by the concepts of heredity (Claude Bernard), social manichaeism and idealistic socialism, resonate with those of Nadar, Manet and subsequently Flaubert.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2148379931355451369" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

Ils s\'inquiètent de le voir tromper son ennui par un projet de défrichement des Landes qui le conduirait à une sorte d\'exil, et parmi tous ceux qui travaillent à son retour en grâce la plus active est la troublante Clorinde qu\'il a refusé d\'épouser.\n\nDans la grande fresque des Rougon-Macquart, Son Excellence Eugène Rougon, que Zola fait paraître en 1876, est le roman du pouvoir et des solidarités d\'intérêts qui appellent l\'intrigue dans le grand monde de Paris où s\'ourdissent les manoeuvres qui font et défont les carrières.
